Page 19

Social News

World Grain Exhibition Conference

Canada Taking the Initiative

From July 25th to August 6th, 1932, Regina, Canada, will host a world "Grain Exhibition Conference." Its scope is extremely broad and of great importance to the future of global agriculture. The following is a description of its origins and content:

The idea for the World Grain Exhibition Conference began three or four years ago, and by 1928, it had gradually matured. Canadian Minister of Agriculture, Mr. Motherwell, convened a representative conference, which unanimously decided to prepare for the general assembly. The aim is to bring together agricultural authorities from around the world, especially those specializing in grain, to exchange ideas and discuss various related issues. At the same time, it will collect and exhibit grains from around the world for a friendly and open competition.

Regina is located in the heart of Canada's largest agricultural region, and 1932 marks the 50th anniversary of the first farmer settlements in Western Canada. This anniversary will also be celebrated, making it an ideal location and time for the conference.

After the plan was announced, it received support and praise from all sides. Canadian Prime Minister King stated, "This undertaking has global implications. Canada, as one of the world's largest grain producers, with millions of acres of land yet to be developed, feels the need to hold a world conference to discuss these issues." Minister of Agriculture Motherwell said, "This conference can encourage interest in improving grain quality and disseminate knowledge about improving general agricultural conditions, with extraordinary potential." Many Canadian newspapers also praised it, such as "Agricultural Report," which said, "This can be a powerful advertisement for our Canada and Canadian products to the world, while also focusing domestic attention on grain fields, which are the foundation of Canadian agriculture."

The conference will be chaired by the Governor-General of Canada, Viscount Willingdon. The Canadian federal government, provincial governments, and national public organizations are all providing support. The Canadian government has sent invitations to countries around the world, inviting them to send representatives and exhibits to participate in the conference. Although the conference date is still two years away, due to the broad scope and the time required for preparation, the Canadian government is actively preparing in all areas. The conference content is divided into two parts: exhibition and conference. There are no restrictions on the year in which the exhibited agricultural products were grown, but preference is given to those grown in 1930 and 1931, as all exhibits must be transported to the venue before March 1, 1932. On the conference side, in order to obtain the best possible results, it is quite difficult to solicit opinions from all parties and draft proposals.

The various detailed rules for managing the exhibition and conference are still being drafted, but the outline has been agreed upon. Generally, the right to send exhibits to the conference for display is granted to agriculturalists from anywhere in the world. Each agriculturalist can register as many types of crops as they have, without restriction, but only one registration can be made for each type. There is no limit on the year in which each exhibit was grown. The registration fee for each type is two dollars if the total value of the exhibit is two thousand dollars or less, and five dollars if the total value exceeds two thousand dollars. Each exhibit must genuinely be the product of the exhibitor and grown under cultivated conditions.

To facilitate the transportation of exhibits, Regina will be declared a port of entry.

The prize money (cash) for competitions in wheat, barley, corn, rye, buckwheat, millet, beans, hemp, sunflowers, radishes, etc., that has already been proposed exceeds 200,500 dollars (equivalent to over 50,000 pounds). The exhibits are divided into 19 categories, further subdivided into 55 classes, with a total of 1,600 prizes ranging from 2,500 dollars to 10 dollars.

Mr. Newman, a grain expert from the Canadian government, serves as the chairman of the prize committee. Other judges are selected from world-renowned experts.

The purpose of exhibiting the products is to demonstrate the progress made in the production and promotion of various agricultural crops through education, exploration, and economic research. The committee of each exhibiting country can freely decide the best way to display each exhibit from a national perspective.

Each country's exhibit can have a free area of 15 feet square. Outside of this area, there is a charge of five dollars for every five feet of frontage. Arrangements for the venue layout must be made with the exhibition authorities as early as possible. A small area will also be opened in the venue for commercial exhibitions. For details, please write to the organizing secretary.

In addition to the exhibition section, the conference section is also very important. Agricultural experts from all countries, whether practical or scientific, are invited to participate in the hope that this conference will become a clearinghouse for the best ideas and knowledge in agriculture worldwide.

To facilitate discussion, the conference will be divided into several sections, meeting in the mornings of each exhibition day. Lantern slides and films will be prepared in English and French as the official languages, and skilled interpreters will be available. The topics for discussion are divided into six categories:

(A) Agriculture

Soils (including physics, chemistry, and mycology)

Cultivation methods, fertilizers, weeds, plant growth and development, experimental methods, seed production and registration, irrigation

(B) Pests and Diseases

(C) Economics (including promotion)

Farm management and costs, grading, sales and investment, storage and transportation, world markets

(D) Milling and Baking

(E) Agricultural Machinery

Cleaning, cultivation, harvesting

(F) General

If national committees have suggestions, they can be submitted at the conference. All proposals and documents will be printed in both English and French and distributed to the representatives before each meeting. Therefore, all proposals must be submitted to the conference executive department before January 1, 1932.

In the future, after the conference, all documents, speeches, and records will be printed into a formal report and distributed to those in all countries who are concerned about agriculture for reference.

Two German Oriental Scholars

Died Simultaneously

Discoverers and Translators of the Turpan Script

Professor Albest von Le Coq and Professor F. W. K. Mueller, famous German oriental scholars, known in academic circles for jointly discovering the Turpan script, suddenly passed away in Berlin within minutes of each other. Those who heard the news were deeply saddened.

Professor Le Coq's reputation began with the success of his Turpan (in Central Asia) expedition. As a result of this expedition, the professor announced to the world the discovery of the remains of an ancient civilization, proving the existence of a link between ancient Greek, Persian, Indian, and Chinese cultures. This discovery is one of the most important discoveries of modern times and is highly relevant to antiquarian exploration. The professor brought the discovered texts and paintings to India, endured many hardships, crossed many high mountains in Central Asia, some of which were 15,000 feet above sea level, and finally arrived in Berlin. The discovered paintings are treasured in the Museum of Anthropology. The professor has written many accounts of his discovery, the most important of which is "Ancient Buddhist Art in Central Asia," a magnificent work in four volumes.

Professor Mueller served as the Director of the Oriental Department of the Berlin Museum of Anthropology for many years and was an authority on Oriental languages and cultures. His exploration area can be said to extend from Syria to Japan, from Japan to the South Pacific Islands, and from the South Pacific Islands back to Syria. He translated Samoan texts, compiled collections of Malay art, and spent many years translating Siamese texts into Western languages. In his later years, he specialized in the study of Chinese and Japanese characters. When Professor Le Coq brought the discovered Turpan documents back to Berlin, he immediately used his knowledge to study the reading of the Turpan script. Therefore, although the credit for the discovery belongs to Le Coq, the credit for the translation belongs to Mueller.

Before the deaths of Professors Le Coq and Mueller, Professor Wilhelm, the Director of the Chinese Cultural Institute in Frankfurt, also passed away. This is a major blow to Oriental studies in Germany. The successor to Professor Wilhelm has not yet been decided and is currently being handled by Mr. Schüler, who is also organizing his posthumous works.

Guidelines for Social News Submissions

(1) Articles should be limited to news about domestic and foreign science, industry, commerce, rural areas, hygiene, aviation, exploration, customs, or urban life.

(2) Illustrations should be limited to various lyrical paintings about social life.

(3) Photographs of seasonal scenery, flora and fauna, people, and newsworthy events are acceptable. Seasonal landscape photography with beautiful women is especially welcome.

(4) Each article should not exceed 2,000 words.

(5) Do not write on both sides of the paper.

(6) Do not write the manuscript horizontally, and the characters should not be cursive. Western text should be omitted where possible. If it is necessary to quote the original text, it must be attached below the translation.

(7) Do not use blue ink to draw illustrations, and do not use colored paper. Use white paper and black ink.

(8) Please indicate that submissions should be addressed to the Social News Department.

(9) Submissions will not be returned regardless of whether they are published or not. If you need them returned, please state this in advance and attach sufficient postage.

(10) Compensation for articles is divided into three levels: three yuan, two yuan, and one yuan per thousand words. The same applies to illustrations and photographs, but special submissions will be compensated separately.

Announcement

(1) Please indicate that submissions from various businesses, companies, doctors, film theaters, and artists should be addressed to the Shanghai Market Department.

(2) Submissions from various businesses, film companies, theaters, artists, and doctors must be stamped for verification; otherwise, they will not be published.

(3) It has been customary to pay a small fee for lengthy articles about films.

The Story of World Literature (1)(6)(6) by John Macy, Translated by Hu Zhongchi

Chapter 42: 19th Century Russian Literature

Turgenev's novels are complete and restrained, while the more intense and unrestrained passions of Fyodor Dostoevsky pour out from within him and do not enter into any model beyond the realm of lawless and irregular facts. In his youth, he was arrested and sent to Siberia for four years for revolutionary activities (which barely went beyond lecturing). His bitter experience was turned into a story in "Memoirs from the House of the Dead." That iron-like experience entered his soul, and he became a sad man for life. However, along with his suffering, and perhaps as a result of it, came a great compassion for all who suffer, for criminals and vagrants, for "the insulted and injured people," as one of his novels is called. His most beautiful work, "Crime and Punishment," is a terrifyingly profound thing. Raskolnikov, a poor student, commits a brutal murder. The motive is not ordinary motives such as jealousy, revenge, or robbery, but the complexities of morbid egoism and resentment towards life. He confesses to his lover, Sonya, a miserable street girl, and she urges him to atone for his sins. He surrenders to the police and is sent to Siberia. Sonya later goes there to follow him. Her dedication is in his atonement. In the suffering of these two children, Dostoevsky, who is not a symbolist, sees all of Russia and dreams of a future in which all of Russia and the world are redeemed by love. He does not believe that the way to cure oppression is violence. In "Demons," he shows the folly and tragedy of revolutionary conspiracy. He believes that the peasants are the hope of Russia, endowing them with virtues that Maxim Gorky now tells us were only the literary and political ideals of that era.

The sorrow of life that flows through all of Dostoevsky's novels is often misunderstood by Western readers as morbid, as limited to humanity having some kind of unhealthy motive. This is morbid, but even so, the novelist should not be blamed any more than a journalist should be blamed for reporting murder cases, fires, abductions on the front page of the newspaper, and also reporting beautiful articles of human courage, such as the death of a polar flight or a surgeon who sacrificed his life in an effort to seek help for several of his compatriots. This is not to say that Dostoevsky took materials from the news; he took materials from universal life. His greatest novel, "The Brothers Karamazov," deals with the questions of good and evil that appear in the weaknesses and difficulties of all unimportant people. There is no "protagonist" in the whole of Dostoevsky, and the heroines are not made for the movies. They are too sad, too tragic, too real. The only thing lacking in Dostoevsky's genius is a sense of humor. Turgenev has it, with a rather pale philosophical and sympathetic nature. Tolstoy, the greatest of them, only has serious and tortuous humor, only smiling and no laughter. There is not much comic relief in Russian literature between Gogol and Chekhov.

Russians are often difficult to understand, but no more difficult than understanding the rest of humanity. Some people think that the Slavic temperament, which is mistakenly called "psychological," can be seen in our close neighbors. In this regard, Dostoevsky's first novel, "Poor Folk," seems to have a strong flavor of Dickens, whose works he actually hardly knew, which is very meaningful. This is a small, round world, but Dostoevsky thought that the alienation of his own people and his own literature from Europe was shown in his wonderful comments on Tolstoy's "Anna Karenina," saying that it was the Russian capital investing a great thing in the ability to solve the problems that oppress humanity. This is the clearest evidence that Russia can offer to the Western world. The problems that oppress humanity have not been solved by novels, whether in the East or the West. The same is true of the problems of human nature, which are suggested and revealed by novels, but not solved. What comes to us from Russia through Dostoevsky is the ability to convey and evoke emotions, such as in "The Brothers Karamazov," and to press interest into the scattered, small, and rather solid fundamental materials. He was not known in England and America until at least the names of Turgenev and Tolstoy were heard. His strong nationalism hindered him at the border, but his humanitarian spirit eventually triumphed among at least conscious people in all countries. Now Dostoevsky is one of the universally recognized great masters of the nineteenth century.

Russian novelists carry the weight of the world on their shoulders. We cannot say whether that burden has enhanced them as artists. Suffice it to say that they are powerful, their art is noble and honest. When Tolstoy died in 1910, he was the most outstanding writer in the world. If he were just a novelist, he would not have been so universally respected. If he had not been a novelist, his identity as a reformer and fighter for freedom would not have been so lofty. There have been many writers who bravely used their pens, their humanity, and their souls in non-literary struggles, such as Milton and Hugo, some of whom sacrificed their energy for "isms" or occasionally put aside their art, and some who, like Byron, abandoned the pen and took up the sword. Tolstoy was probably the only artist of first-rate creativity who tried to deny and suppress his art as a demon or irrelevant thing for his higher purposes. Fortunately, those purposes did not completely occupy his heart until his creative impulses had already been expressed in his novels and stories. In his youth, he was a hereditary aristocrat and soldier, and was hereditary not in nature but in outward affairs. His experiences in the Crimean War provided material for "Sevastopol" and other war stories. Those works immediately brought him fame and established his literary career. The nature of those works, the masters of those works, were, as he himself said, true and without false exaggeration or sentimental traces of glory. He saw war as it really was, as a terrible and useless thing in which people displayed simple, blind courage. At that time, he was neither a pacifist nor a propagandist. In fact, the emperor ordered him to retreat from the dangerous area. Even after his fame was resounding and certain, he was more interested in people and things than in literature, and that was the source of his power. He spent his time in the countryside on the manor, striving to educate the village children and improve the conditions of the farmers. In the midst of many activities, he wrote his two great novels, "War and Peace" and "Anna Karenina."

Peicheng Girls to

Perform The Fan of Mrs. Errant

Recently, the enthusiasm of Shanghai people for performing plays is indeed as rampant as fire. The Drama Association has just finished performing "The Merchant of Venice," and the Xinyou Drama Society is following up with "Uncle Vanya." The Fudan Drama Society's "Cyrano" and the Nanguo Society's "Carmen" are also about to be staged in succession. The young ladies of Peicheng Girls' School,熏陶ed by such hot artistic air, are also happily organizing drama troupes and working hard every day. As a result, they have decided to perform "The Fan of Mrs. Errant." Regarding this script, there is no need for any introduction. Everyone knows that it is a translation by Mr. Hong Shen. The Drama Association performed it a few years ago with Ms. Tang Ying in the lead role. Last year, Tsinghua University in Peiping and Nankai University in Tianjin also performed it. This time, the ladies of Peicheng, in order to shine in the center of this theatrical movement, are paying great attention to the selection of personnel, especially the selection of the protagonist, Ms. Xu Errant. They spent several days deciding to invite Miss Chen Minde, a woman of great talent and beauty, to take on the role. It is said that Miss Chen grew up in the north and speaks fluent Mandarin, both cleverly and purely. Moreover, Miss Chen's graceful figure is also truly in line with the identity of the character in the play. Their performance date is June 16th, and the location is the Summer Peake Cinema. Tickets are divided into one yuan and two yuan types. In addition to the young ladies selling them themselves, various universities and major bookstores are also selling them on commission. I think that in such hot weather, watching The Fan of Mrs. Errant is more worthwhile than drinking a few bottles of soda and eating a few pieces of ice cream.

The Cherry Blossom Drama Society of National Central University

Chen Xianrong

Many universities in our country have drama society organizations (referring to most). National Central University is the highest institution of learning in the capital, so it cannot escape being an exception. However, which is the drama society of National Central University? It is the Cherry Blossom Drama Society.

The Cherry Blossom Drama Society is the only organization at National Central University that studies drama. She has a long and glorious history. She is a beautiful, rich, and sympathetic art society full of beauty and love, giving us the sweet spring of life and the relics of the soul. Her instructor is Mr. Zhao Boyan, whom we have always admired. Mr. Zhao is an associate professor at National Central University, specializing in drama. When he studied in Japan and Germany, he directed several new dramas, and the audience admired his reputation. After he took the position at National Central University, the Cherry Blossom Drama Society was established in the school, and he was invited to be the instructor. He compiled and translated the Austrian drama "The Taste of Love," but it was not formally rehearsed. The characters and plots of other dramas directed by him were all appropriate. Therefore, the Cherry Blossom Drama Society became famous at the time. Now Mr. Zhao has passed away. The performance on New Year's Day the year before last was directed by him during his lifetime. At that time, the performance address was borrowing the Lu shed recently erected by Zhongda. Its layout was the shed on the west was the theater, and the two sides were the dressing room and the storage room. Suspended outside the shed was a circle of cypress, with the words Cherry Blossom Theater. The shed was decorated with many colored paper strips, and the inside was wrapped with green cypress branches. In the center of the venue was a flower bed with a bright electric torch suspended above it. Colored paper was also radiating from the corners of the roof. In addition, the scenery was based on a simple and solemn spirit. Dark cloths were suspended on both sides in three rows, and gray cloths were horizontally tied along the three layers. The back history used sky blue as the background, and the white electric light shone straight from both sides, which was extremely elegant. The results of the performances were all quite successful, and we cannot but attribute this to Mr. Zhao.

Now, although Mr. Zhao is dead, the comrades of the Cherry Blossom Society have not been discouraged by this, let alone become negative. It is reported that they have recently held a large-scale recruitment of members, and there are already many male and female students who sincerely request the board of directors of the society to introduce them into the society.

Guidelines for Youth Forum Submissions

(1) Articles should be limited to discussions about youth issues, domestic and foreign school or student news, essays, descriptions of school life, etc., and the style is not limited.

(2) Illustrations should be limited to those related to youth issues.

(3) Photographs of youth life (such as sports, travel, excursions, drama, lectures, gatherings, exhibitions, various competitions, etc.), school news, and youth movement photography are acceptable. Both individuals and groups are welcome.

(4) Do not write on both sides of the paper.

(5) Each article should not exceed 2,000 words.

(6) Do not write the manuscript horizontally, and the characters should not be cursive. Western text should be omitted where possible. If it is necessary to quote the original text, it must be attached below the translation.
